style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>2</span>)</li>\n<li><span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" (He)</li>\n<li><span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6998150000000000000&#9824;</span>1.5%<span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em;">&#177;</span>0.5%</span> <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> (CH<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>4</span>)</li>\n<li><span class="nowrap">~0.019% <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_deuteride" title="Hydrogen deuteride">hydrogen deuteride</a> (HD)</span></li>\n<li>~0.00015% <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a> (C<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>2</span>H<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>6</span>)</li>\n<li><b>Ices</b>:</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> (NH<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>3</span>)</li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_(molecule)" title="Water (molecule)">water</a> (H<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>2</span>O)</li>\n<li><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Ammonium_hydrosulfide" title="Ammonium hydrosulfide">ammonium hydrosulfide</a> (NH<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>4</span>SH)</span></li>\n<li><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Methane_clathrate" title="Methane clathrate">methane ice</a> (?) (CH<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br/>4</span>&#160;<span style="font-weight:bold;">&#183;</span> 5.75H<sub>2</sub>O)</span></li></ul>\n</div></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n</div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Neptune</b> is the eighth and farthest known <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. In the Solar System, it is the fourth-largest planet by diameter, the third-most-massive planet, and the densest <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">giant planet</a>. Neptune is 17 times the mass of <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> and is slightly more massive than its near-twin <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>, which is 15 times the mass of Earth and slightly larger than Neptune.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mass2_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-mass2-17">[d]</a></sup> Neptune orbits the Sun once every 164.8&#160;<a href="/wiki/Julian_year_(astronomy)" title="Julian year (astronomy)">years</a> at an average distance of 30.1&#160;<a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a> (4.5&#160;billion&#160;km). It is named after the <a href="/wiki/Neptune_(mythology)" title="Neptune (mythology)">Roman god of the sea</a> and has the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astronomical_symbol" title="Astronomical symbol">astronomical symbol</a> &#9798;, a stylised version of the god Neptune\'s <a href="/wiki/Trident" title="Trident">trident</a>.\n</p><p>Neptune is not visible to the unaided eye and is the only planet in the Solar System found by mathematical prediction rather than by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empirical_observation" title="Empirical observation">empirical observation</a>. Unexpected changes in the orbit of Uranus led <a href="/wiki/Alexis_Bouvard" title="Alexis Bouvard">Alexis Bouvard</a> to deduce that its orbit was subject to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">gravitational</a> <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_(astronomy)" title="Perturbation (astronomy)">perturbation</a> by an unknown planet. Neptune was subsequently observed with a telescope on 23 September 1846<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamilton_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hamilton-1">[1]</a></sup> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Johann_Galle" title="Johann Galle">Johann Galle</a> within a <a href="/wiki/Degree_(angle)" title="Degree (angle)">degree</a> of the position predicted by <a href="/wiki/Urbain_Le_Verrier" title="Urbain Le Verrier">Urbain Le Verrier</a>. Its largest moon, <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a>, was discovered shortly thereafter, though none of the planet\'s remaining known 13 <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Neptune" title="Moons of Neptune">moons</a> were located telescopically until the 20th century. The planet\'s distance from Earth gives it a very small apparent size, making it challenging to study with Earth-based telescopes. Neptune was visited by <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a></i>, when it flew by the planet on 25 August 1989.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20141018-KC_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20141018-KC-18">[14]</a></sup> The advent of the <i><a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a></i> and large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ground-based_telescope" title="Ground-based telescope">ground-based telescopes</a> with <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_optics" title="Adaptive optics">adaptive optics</a> has recently allowed for additional detailed observations from afar.\n</p><p>Like Jupiter and Saturn, Neptune\'s atmosphere is composed primarily of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>, along with traces of <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a> and possibly <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a>, though it contains a higher proportion of "ices" such as water, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>. However, similar to Uranus, its interior is primarily composed of ices and rock;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak_Weizman_et_al._1995-19">[15]</a></sup> Uranus and Neptune are normally considered "<a href="/wiki/Ice_giant" title="Ice giant">ice giants</a>" to emphasise this distinction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-20">[16]</a></sup> Traces of methane in the outermost regions in part account for the planet\'s blue appearance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bluecolour_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-bluecolour-21">[17]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In contrast to the hazy, relatively featureless atmosphere of Uranus, Neptune\'s atmosphere has active and visible weather patterns. For example, at the time of the <i>Voyager 2</i> <a href="/wiki/Planetary_flyby" title="Planetary flyby">flyby</a> in 1989, the planet\'s southern hemisphere had a <a href="/wiki/Great_Dark_Spot" title="Great Dark Spot">Great Dark Spot</a> comparable to the <a href="/wiki/Great_Red_Spot" title="Great Red Spot">Great Red Spot</a> on Jupiter. These weather patterns are driven by the strongest sustained winds of any planet in the Solar System, with recorded wind speeds as high as 2,100&#160;km/h (580&#160;m/s; 1,300&#160;mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suomi1991_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Suomi1991-22">[18]</a></sup> Because of its great distance from the Sun, Neptune\'s outer atmosphere is one of the coldest places in the Solar System, with temperatures at its cloud tops approaching 55&#160;<a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a> (&#8722;218&#160;<a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">&#176;C</a>; &#8722;361&#160;<a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit" title="Fahrenheit">&#176;F</a>). Temperatures at the planet\'s centre are approximately 5,400&#160;K (5,100&#160;&#176;C; 9,300&#160;&#176;F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hubbard_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-hubbard-23">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nettelmann_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-nettelmann-24">[20]</a></sup> Neptune has a faint and fragmented <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_ring" title="Planetary ring">ring system</a> (labelled "arcs"), which was discovered in 1984, then later confirmed by <i>Voyager 2</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ring1_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-ring1-25">[21]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">Discovery</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Discovery_of_Neptune" title="Discovery of Neptune">Discovery of Neptune</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1448" data-file-width="1180" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg/170px-Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg/255px-Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg/340px-Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Some of the earliest recorded observations ever made through a <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescope</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo\'s</a> drawings on 28 December 1612 and 27 January 1613 contain plotted points that match up with what is now known to be the position of Neptune. On both occasions, Galileo seems to have mistaken Neptune for a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fixed_star" title="Fixed star">fixed star</a> when it appeared close&#8212;in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conjunction_(astronomy_and_astrology)" title="Conjunction (astronomy and astrology)">conjunction</a>&#8212;to Jupiter in the <a href="/wiki/Night_sky" title="Night sky">night sky</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[22]</a></sup> hence, he is not credited with Neptune\'s discovery. At his first observation in December 1612, Neptune was almost stationary in the sky because it had just turned <a href="/wiki/Apparent_retrograde_motion" title="Apparent retrograde motion">retrograde</a> that day. This apparent backward motion is created when Earth\'s orbit takes it past an outer planet. Because Neptune was only beginning its yearly retrograde cycle, the motion of the planet was far too slight to be detected with Galileo\'s small <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescope</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[23]</a></sup> In July 2009, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Melbourne" title="University of Melbourne">University of Melbourne</a> physicist David Jamieson announced new evidence suggesting that Galileo was at least aware that the "star" he had observed had moved relative to the <a href="/wiki/Fixed_stars" title="Fixed stars">fixed stars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[24]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1821, <a href="/wiki/Alexis_Bouvard" title="Alexis Bouvard">Alexis Bouvard</a> published astronomical tables of the <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbit</a> of Neptune\'s neighbour <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[25]</a></sup> Subsequent observations revealed substantial deviations from the tables, leading Bouvard to hypothesise that an unknown body was <a href="/wiki/Perturbation_(astronomy)" title="Perturbation (astronomy)">perturbing</a> the orbit through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">gravitational</a> interaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MNRAS7_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-MNRAS7-30">[26]</a></sup> In 1843, <a href="/wiki/John_Couch_Adams" title="John Couch Adams">John Couch Adams</a> began work on the orbit of Uranus using the data he had. Via <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Observatory" title="Cambridge Observatory">Cambridge Observatory</a> director <a href="/wiki/James_Challis" title="James Challis">James Challis</a>, he requested extra data from Sir <a href="/wiki/George_Biddell_Airy" title="George Biddell Airy">George Airy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Astronomer_Royal" title="Astronomer Royal">Astronomer Royal</a>, who supplied it in February 1844. Adams continued to work in 1845&#8211;46 and produced several different estimates of a new planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[28]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Urbain_Le_Verrier.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="851" data-file-width="669" decoding="async" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Urbain_Le_Verrier.jpg/170px-Urbain_Le_Verrier.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Urbain_Le_Verrier.jpg/255px-Urbain_Le_Verrier.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Urbain_Le_Verrier.jpg/340px-Urbain_Le_Verrier.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Urbain_Le_Verrier.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Urbain_Le_Verrier" title="Urbain Le Verrier">Urbain Le Verrier</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1845&#8211;46, <a href="/wiki/Urbain_Le_Verrier" title="Urbain Le Verrier">Urbain Le Verrier</a>, independently of Adams, developed his own calculations but aroused no enthusiasm in his compatriots. In June 1846, upon seeing Le Verrier\'s first published estimate of the planet\'s longitude and its similarity to Adams\'s estimate, Airy persuaded Challis to search for the planet. Challis vainly scoured the sky throughout August and September.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MNRAS7_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-MNRAS7-30">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[29]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Meanwhile, Le Verrier by letter urged <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Observatory" title="Berlin Observatory">Berlin Observatory</a> astronomer <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottfried_Galle" title="Johann Gottfried Galle">Johann Gottfried Galle</a> to search with the observatory\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Refractor" title="Refractor">refractor</a>. <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Louis_d%27Arrest" title="Heinrich Louis d\'Arrest">Heinrich d\'Arrest</a>, a student at the observatory, suggested to Galle that they could compare a recently drawn chart of the sky in the region of Le Verrier\'s predicted location with the current sky to seek the displacement characteristic of a <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a>, as opposed to a fixed star. On the evening of 23 September 1846, the day Galle received the letter, he discovered Neptune within 1&#176; of where Le Verrier had predicted it to be, about 12&#176; from Adams\' prediction. Challis later realised that he had observed the planet twice, on 4 and 12 August, but did not recognise it as a planet because he lacked an up-to-date star map and was distracted by his concurrent work on comet observations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MNRAS7_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-MNRAS7-30">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[30]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the wake of the discovery, there was much nationalistic rivalry between the French and the British over who deserved credit for the discovery. Eventually, an international consensus emerged that both Le Verrier and Adams jointly deserved credit. Since 1966, <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Rawlins" title="Dennis Rawlins">Dennis Rawlins</a> has questioned the credibility of Adams\'s claim to co-discovery, and the issue was re-evaluated by historians with the return in 1998 of the "Neptune papers" (historical documents) to the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Observatory,_Greenwich" title="Royal Observatory, Greenwich">Royal Observatory, Greenwich</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Neptdisc_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Neptdisc-35">[31]</a></sup> After reviewing the documents, they suggest that "Adams does not deserve equal credit with Le Verrier for the discovery of Neptune. That credit belongs only to the person who succeeded both in predicting the planet\'s place and in convincing astronomers to search for it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[32]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Naming">Naming</span></h3>\n<p>Shortly after its discovery, Neptune was referred to simply as "the planet exterior to Uranus" or as "Le Verrier\'s planet". The first suggestion for a name came from Galle, who proposed the name <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Janus_(mythology)" title="Janus (mythology)">Janus</a></i>. In England, Challis put forward the name <i><a href="/wiki/Oceanus" title="Oceanus">Oceanus</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[33]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Claiming the right to name his discovery, Le Verrier quickly proposed the name <i>Neptune</i> for this new planet, though falsely stating that this had been officially approved by the French <a href="/wiki/Bureau_des_Longitudes" title="Bureau des Longitudes">Bureau des Longitudes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[34]</a></sup> In October, he sought to name the planet <i>Le Verrier</i>, after himself, and he had loyal support in this from the observatory director, <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Arago" title="Fran&#231;ois Arago">Fran&#231;ois Arago</a>. This suggestion met with stiff resistance outside France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[35]</a></sup> French almanacs quickly reintroduced the name <i>Herschel</i> for Uranus, after that planet\'s discoverer Sir <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel" title="William Herschel">William Herschel</a>, and <i>Leverrier</i> for the new planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[36]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Georg_Wilhelm_von_Struve" title="Friedrich Georg Wilhelm von Struve">Struve</a> came out in favour of the name <i>Neptune</i> on 29 December 1846, to the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Russian Academy of Sciences">Saint Petersburg Academy of Sciences</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[37]</a></sup> Soon, <i>Neptune</i> became the internationally accepted name. In <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">Roman mythology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neptune_(mythology)" title="Neptune (mythology)">Neptune</a> was the god of the sea, identified with the Greek <a href="/wiki/Poseidon" title="Poseidon">Poseidon</a>. The demand for a mythological name seemed to be in keeping with the nomenclature of the other planets, all of which, except for Earth, were named for deities in <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek</a> and Roman mythology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-42">[38]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most languages today use some variant of the name "Neptune" for the planet; indeed in Chinese, Vietnamese, Japanese, and <a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a>, the planet\'s name was translated as "sea king star" (<span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#28023;&#29579;&#26143;</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[40]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_language" title="Mongolian language">Mongolian</a>, Neptune is called <i>Dalain Van</i> (&#1044;&#1072;&#1083;&#1072;&#1081;&#1085; &#1074;&#1072;&#1085;), reflecting its namesake god\'s role as the ruler of the sea. In modern <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> the planet is called <i>Poseidon</i> (&#928;&#959;&#963;&#949;&#953;&#948;&#974;&#957;&#945;&#962;, <i>Poseidonas</i>), the Greek counterpart of Neptune.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[41]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>, "Rahab" (&#1512;&#1492;&#1489;), from <a href="/wiki/Rahab_(Egypt)#Jewish_folklore" title="Rahab (Egypt)">a Biblical sea monster</a> mentioned in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Book_of_Psalms" title="Book of Psalms">Book of Psalms</a>, was selected in a vote managed by the <a href="/wiki/Academy_of_the_Hebrew_Language" title="Academy of the Hebrew Language">Academy of the Hebrew Language</a> in 2009 as the official name for the planet, even though the existing Latin term "Neptun" (&#1504;&#1508;&#1496;&#1493;&#1503;) is commonly used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haaretz_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Haaretz-46">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hayadan.org.il_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hayadan.org.il-47">[43]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_language" title="M&#257;ori language">M&#257;ori</a>, the planet is called <i>Tangaroa</i>, named after the <a href="/wiki/Tangaroa" title="Tangaroa">M&#257;ori god of the sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Appendix_5:_Planetary_Linguistics_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Appendix_5:_Planetary_Linguistics-48">[44]</a></sup> In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nahuatl_language" title="Nahuatl language">Nahuatl</a>, the planet is called <i>Tl&#257;locc&#299;tlalli</i>, named after the rain god <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tlaloc" title="Tlaloc">Tl&#257;loc</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Appendix_5:_Planetary_Linguistics_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Appendix_5:_Planetary_Linguistics-48">[44]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Thai_language" title="Thai language">Thai</a>, Neptune is referred both by its Westernised name <i>Dao Nepjun</i> (&#3604;&#3634;&#3623;&#3648;&#3609;&#3611;&#3592;&#3641;&#3609;), and is also named <i>Dao Ketu</i> (&#3604;&#3634;&#3623;&#3648;&#3585;&#3605;&#3640;, "Star of Ketu"), after the descending <a href="/wiki/Lunar_node" title="Lunar node">lunar node</a> <a href="/wiki/Ketu_(mythology)" title="Ketu (mythology)">Ketu</a> (&#2325;&#2375;&#2340;&#2369;) who plays a role in <a href="/wiki/Hindu_astrology" title="Hindu astrology">Hindu astrology</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Status">Status</span></h3>\n<p>From its discovery in 1846 until the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Discovery_of_Pluto" title="Discovery of Pluto">discovery of Pluto</a> in 1930, Neptune was the farthest known planet. When Pluto was discovered, it was considered a planet, and Neptune thus became the second-farthest known planet, except for a 20-year period between 1979 and 1999 when Pluto\'s elliptical orbit brought it closer than Neptune to the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[45]</a></sup> The discovery of the <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a> in 1992 led many astronomers to debate whether Pluto should be considered a planet or as part of the Kuiper belt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[47]</a></sup> In 2006, the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2006_definition_of_planet" title="2006 definition of planet">defined the word "planet" for the first time</a>, reclassifying Pluto as a "<a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a>" and making Neptune once again the outermost known planet in the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[48]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">Physical characteristics</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Neptune,_Earth_size_comparison_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2251" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Neptune%2C_Earth_size_comparison_2.jpg/220px-Neptune%2C_Earth_size_comparison_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Neptune%2C_Earth_size_comparison_2.jpg/330px-Neptune%2C_Earth_size_comparison_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Neptune%2C_Earth_size_comparison_2.jpg/440px-Neptune%2C_Earth_size_comparison_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Neptune,_Earth_size_comparison_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A size comparison of Neptune and Earth</div></div></div>\n<p>Neptune\'s mass of 1.0243<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">&#215;</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">26</span></sup>&#160;kg<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_4-13"><a href="#cite_note-fact-4">[4]</a></sup> is intermediate between Earth and the larger <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">gas giants</a>: it is 17 times that of Earth but just 1/19th that of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mass_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-mass-53">[e]</a></sup> Its <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a> at 1 bar is 11.15&#160;m/s<sup>2</sup>, 1.14 times the <a href="/wiki/Surface_gravity" title="Surface gravity">surface gravity</a> of Earth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[49]</a></sup> and surpassed only by Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Uns&#246;ld_&amp;_Baschek_2001_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Uns&#246;ld_&amp;_Baschek_2001-55">[50]</a></sup> Neptune\'s <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equatorial</a> radius of 24,764&#160;km<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seidelmann_Archinal_A\'hearn_et_al._2007_8-5"><a href="#cite_note-Seidelmann_Archinal_A\'hearn_et_al._2007-8">[7]</a></sup> is nearly four times that of Earth. Neptune, like <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>, is an <a href="/wiki/Ice_giant" title="Ice giant">ice giant</a>, a subclass of <a href="/wiki/Giant_planet" title="Giant planet">giant planet</a>, because they are smaller and have higher concentrations of <a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">volatiles</a> than Jupiter and <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boss_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boss-56">[51]</a></sup> In the search for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extrasolar_planet" title="Extrasolar planet">extrasolar planets</a>, Neptune has been used as a <a href="/wiki/Metonymy" title="Metonymy">metonym</a>: discovered bodies of similar mass are often referred to as "Neptunes",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[52]</a></sup> just as scientists refer to various extrasolar bodies as "Jupiters".\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_structure">Internal structure</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_diamonds" title="Extraterrestrial diamonds">Extraterrestrial diamonds</a></div>\n<p>Neptune\'s internal structure resembles that of <a href="/wiki/Uranus#Internal_structure" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>. Its atmosphere forms about 5% to 10% of its mass and extends perhaps 10% to 20% of the way towards the core, where it reaches pressures of about 10&#160;<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">GPa</a>, or about 100,000 times that of Earth\'s atmosphere. Increasing concentrations of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> and water are found in the lower regions of the atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hubbard_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-hubbard-23">[19]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Neptune_diagram.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1300" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Neptune_diagram.svg/220px-Neptune_diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Neptune_diagram.svg/330px-Neptune_diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Neptune_diagram.svg/440px-Neptune_diagram.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Neptune_diagram.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The internal structure of Neptune: <div><ol><li>Upper atmosphere, top clouds</li><li>Atmosphere consisting of hydrogen, helium and methane gas</li><li>Mantle consisting of water, ammonia and methane ices</li><li>Core consisting of rock (silicates and nickel&#8211;iron)</li></ol></div></div></div></div>\n<p>The mantle is equivalent to 10 to 15 Earth masses and is rich in water, ammonia and methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamilton_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hamilton-1">[1]</a></sup> As is customary in planetary science, this mixture is referred to as <a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">icy</a> even though it is a hot, dense fluid. This fluid, which has a high electrical conductivity, is sometimes called a water&#8211;ammonia ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atreya2006_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Atreya2006-58">[53]</a></sup> The mantle may consist of a layer of ionic water in which the water molecules break down into a soup of hydrogen and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxygen_ion" title="Oxygen ion">oxygen ions</a>, and deeper down <a href="/wiki/Superionic_water" title="Superionic water">superionic water</a> in which the oxygen crystallises but the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_ion" title="Hydrogen ion">hydrogen ions</a> float around freely within the oxygen lattice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[54]</a></sup> At a depth of 7,000&#160;km, the conditions may be such that methane decomposes into diamond crystals that rain downwards like hailstones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WP-20170825_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-WP-20170825-61">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[57]</a></sup> Very-high-pressure experiments at the <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Livermore_National_Laboratory" title="Lawrence Livermore National Laboratory">Lawrence Livermore National Laboratory</a> suggest that the top of the mantle may be an ocean of liquid carbon with floating solid \'diamonds\'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eggert_2004_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eggert_2004-64">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eggert_2010_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eggert_2010-65">[60]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Planetary_core" title="Planetary core">core</a> of Neptune is likely composed of iron, nickel and <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicates</a>, with an interior model giving a mass about 1.2 times that of Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pass43_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-pass43-66">[61]</a></sup> The pressure at the centre is 7&#160;<a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">Mbar</a> (700 GPa), about twice as high as that at the centre of Earth, and the temperature may be 5,400&#160;K.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hubbard_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-hubbard-23">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nettelmann_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-nettelmann-24">[20]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmosphere">Atmosphere</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Neptune-Methane.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="380" data-file-width="540" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Neptune-Methane.jpg/220px-Neptune-Methane.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Neptune-Methane.jpg/330px-Neptune-Methane.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Neptune-Methane.jpg/440px-Neptune-Methane.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Neptune-Methane.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Combined colour and near-<a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> image of Neptune, showing bands of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> in its <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>, and four of its <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Proteus_(moon)" title="Proteus (moon)">Proteus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Larissa_(moon)" title="Larissa (moon)">Larissa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galatea_(moon)" title="Galatea (moon)">Galatea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Despina_(moon)" title="Despina (moon)">Despina</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload=\'&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:640px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1"
