data-framerate="30"/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;\'><img alt="File:Neptune\'s Dynamic Environment.webm" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Neptune%27s_Dynamic_Environment.webm/220px--Neptune%27s_Dynamic_Environment.webm.jpg" style="width:220px;height:165px"/><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b3/Neptune%27s_Dynamic_Environment.webm" target="new" title="Play media"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Neptune%27s_Dynamic_Environment.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A time-lapse video of Neptune and its moons</div></div></div>\n<p>At high altitudes, Neptune\'s atmosphere is 80% <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and 19% <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hubbard_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-hubbard-23">[19]</a></sup> A trace amount of methane is also present. Prominent absorption bands of methane exist at wavelengths above 600&#160;nm, in the red and infrared portion of the spectrum. As with Uranus, this absorption of red light by the <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_methane" title="Atmospheric methane">atmospheric methane</a> is part of what gives Neptune its blue hue,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[62]</a></sup> although Neptune\'s vivid azure differs from Uranus\'s milder <a href="/wiki/Cyan" title="Cyan">cyan</a>. Because Neptune\'s atmospheric methane content is similar to that of Uranus, some unknown atmospheric constituent is thought to contribute to Neptune\'s colour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bluecolour_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-bluecolour-21">[17]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Neptune\'s atmosphere is subdivided into two main regions: the lower <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a>, where temperature decreases with altitude, and the <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">stratosphere</a>, where temperature increases with altitude. The boundary between the two, the <a href="/wiki/Tropopause" title="Tropopause">tropopause</a>, lies at a pressure of 0.1 bars (10&#160;kPa).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-20">[16]</a></sup> The stratosphere then gives way to the <a href="/wiki/Thermosphere" title="Thermosphere">thermosphere</a> at a pressure lower than 10<sup>&#8722;5</sup> to 10<sup>&#8722;4</sup> bars (1 to 10&#160;Pa).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-20">[16]</a></sup> The thermosphere gradually transitions to the <a href="/wiki/Exosphere" title="Exosphere">exosphere</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Neptune_clouds.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="596" data-file-width="682" decoding="async" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Neptune_clouds.jpg/220px-Neptune_clouds.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Neptune_clouds.jpg/330px-Neptune_clouds.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Neptune_clouds.jpg/440px-Neptune_clouds.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Neptune_clouds.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bands of high-altitude clouds cast shadows on Neptune\'s lower cloud deck</div></div></div>\n<p>Models suggest that Neptune\'s troposphere is banded by clouds of varying compositions depending on altitude. The upper-level clouds lie at pressures below one bar, where the temperature is suitable for methane to condense. For pressures between one and five bars (100 and 500 kPa), clouds of ammonia and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> are thought to form. Above a pressure of five bars, the clouds may consist of ammonia, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ammonium_sulfide" title="Ammonium sulfide">ammonium sulfide</a>, hydrogen sulfide and water. Deeper clouds of water ice should be found at pressures of about 50 bars (5.0&#160;MPa), where the temperature reaches 273&#160;K (0&#160;&#176;C). Underneath, clouds of ammonia and hydrogen sulfide may be found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elkins-tanton_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-elkins-tanton-68">[63]</a></sup>\n</p><p>High-altitude clouds on Neptune have been observed casting shadows on the opaque cloud deck below. There are also high-altitude cloud bands that wrap around the planet at constant latitude. These circumferential bands have widths of 50&#8211;150&#160;km and lie about 50&#8211;110&#160;km above the cloud deck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apj125_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-apj125-69">[64]</a></sup> These altitudes are in the layer where weather occurs, the troposphere. Weather does not occur in the higher stratosphere or thermosphere. Unlike Uranus, Neptune\'s composition has a higher volume of ocean, whereas Uranus has a smaller mantle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[65]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Neptune\'s <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">spectra</a> suggest that its lower stratosphere is hazy due to condensation of products of ultraviolet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photolysis" title="Photolysis">photolysis</a> of methane, such as <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">ethyne</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-20">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hubbard_23-4"><a href="#cite_note-hubbard-23">[19]</a></sup> The stratosphere is also home to trace amounts of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_cyanide" title="Hydrogen cyanide">hydrogen cyanide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_20-4"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-20">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encrenaz_2003_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encrenaz_2003-71">[66]</a></sup> The stratosphere of Neptune is warmer than that of Uranus due to the elevated concentration of hydrocarbons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_20-5"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-20">[16]</a></sup>\n</p><p>For reasons that remain obscure, the planet\'s thermosphere is at an anomalously high temperature of about 750&#160;K.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Broadfoot19989_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Broadfoot19989-72">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herbert_&amp;_Sandel_1999_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Herbert_&amp;_Sandel_1999-73">[68]</a></sup> The planet is too far from the Sun for this heat to be generated by <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> radiation. One candidate for a heating mechanism is atmospheric interaction with ions in the planet\'s <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetic field</a>. Other candidates are <a href="/wiki/Gravity_wave" title="Gravity wave">gravity waves</a> from the interior that dissipate in the atmosphere. The <a href="/wiki/Thermosphere" title="Thermosphere">thermosphere</a> contains traces of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and water, which may have been deposited from external sources such as <a href="/wiki/Meteorite" title="Meteorite">meteorites</a> and dust.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elkins-tanton_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-elkins-tanton-68">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encrenaz_2003_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Encrenaz_2003-71">[66]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetosphere">Magnetosphere</span></h3>\n<p>Neptune resembles Uranus in its <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a>, with a <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a> strongly tilted relative to its <a href="/wiki/Rotation" title="Rotation">rotational</a> axis at 47&#176; and offset at least 0.55&#160;radii, or about 13,500&#160;km from the planet\'s physical centre. Before <i>Voyager 2</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> arrival at Neptune, it was hypothesised that Uranus\'s tilted magnetosphere was the result of its sideways rotation. In comparing the magnetic fields of the two planets, scientists now think the extreme orientation may be characteristic of flows in the planets\' interiors. This field may be generated by <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convective</a> fluid motions in a thin spherical shell of\n<a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">electrically conducting</a> liquids (probably a combination of ammonia, methane and water)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elkins-tanton_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-elkins-tanton-68">[63]</a></sup> resulting in a <a href="/wiki/Dynamo" title="Dynamo">dynamo</a> action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[69]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The dipole component of the magnetic field at the magnetic equator of Neptune is about 14&#160;<a href="/wiki/Tesla_(unit)" title="Tesla (unit)">microteslas</a> (0.14&#160;<a href="/wiki/Gauss_(unit)" title="Gauss (unit)">G</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Connerney1991_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Connerney1991-75">[70]</a></sup> The dipole <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_moment" title="Magnetic moment">magnetic moment</a> of Neptune is about 2.2&#160;&#215;&#160;10<sup>17</sup>&#160;T&#183;m<sup>3</sup> (14&#160;&#956;T&#183;<i>R</i><sub><i>N</i></sub><sup>3</sup>, where <i>R</i><sub><i>N</i></sub> is the radius of Neptune). Neptune\'s magnetic field has a complex geometry that includes relatively large contributions from non-dipolar components, including a strong <a href="/wiki/Quadrupole" title="Quadrupole">quadrupole</a> moment that may exceed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_dipole_moment" title="Magnetic dipole moment">dipole moment</a> in strength. By contrast, Earth, Jupiter and Saturn have only relatively small quadrupole moments, and their fields are less tilted from the polar axis. The large quadrupole moment of Neptune may be the result of offset from the planet\'s centre and geometrical constraints of the field\'s dynamo generator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science4936_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-science4936-76">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[72]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Neptune\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bow_shock" title="Bow shock">bow shock</a>, where the magnetosphere begins to slow the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>, occurs at a distance of 34.9 times the radius of the planet. The <a href="/wiki/Magnetopause" title="Magnetopause">magnetopause</a>, where the pressure of the magnetosphere counterbalances the solar wind, lies at a distance of 23&#8211;26.5 times the radius of Neptune. The tail of the magnetosphere extends out to at least 72 times the radius of Neptune, and likely much farther.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science4936_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-science4936-76">[71]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Neptune_storms.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="870" data-file-width="945" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Neptune_storms.jpg/220px-Neptune_storms.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Neptune_storms.jpg/330px-Neptune_storms.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Neptune_storms.jpg/440px-Neptune_storms.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Neptune_storms.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Dark_Spot" title="Great Dark Spot">Great Dark Spot</a> (top), Scooter (middle white cloud),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scooter_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-scooter-78">[73]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Small_Dark_Spot" title="Small Dark Spot">Small Dark Spot</a> (bottom), with contrast exaggerated.</div></div></div>\n<p>Neptune\'s weather is characterised by extremely dynamic storm systems, with winds reaching speeds of almost 600&#160;m/s (2,200&#160;km/h; 1,300&#160;mph)&#8212;nearly reaching <a href="/wiki/Supersonic_speed" title="Supersonic speed">supersonic</a> flow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suomi1991_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Suomi1991-22">[18]</a></sup> More typically, by tracking the motion of persistent clouds, wind speeds have been shown to vary from 20&#160;m/s in the easterly direction to 325&#160;m/s westward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammel1989_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hammel1989-79">[74]</a></sup> At the cloud tops, the prevailing winds range in speed from 400&#160;m/s along the equator to 250&#160;m/s at the poles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elkins-tanton_68-3"><a href="#cite_note-elkins-tanton-68">[63]</a></sup> Most of the winds on Neptune move in a direction opposite the planet\'s rotation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burgess2_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-burgess2-80">[75]</a></sup> The general pattern of winds showed prograde rotation at high latitudes vs. retrograde rotation at lower latitudes. The difference in flow direction is thought to be a "skin effect" and not due to any deeper atmospheric processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_20-6"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-20">[16]</a></sup> At 70&#176; S latitude, a high-speed jet travels at a speed of 300&#160;m/s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_20-7"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-20">[16]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Neptune differs from Uranus in its typical level of meteorological activity. <i>Voyager 2</i> observed weather phenomena on Neptune during its 1989 flyby,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spot_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-spot-81">[76]</a></sup> but no comparable phenomena on Uranus during its 1986 fly-by.\n</p><p>The abundance of methane, ethane and <a href="/wiki/Acetylene" title="Acetylene">acetylene</a> at Neptune\'s equator is 10&#8211;100 times greater than at the poles. This is interpreted as evidence for upwelling at the equator and subsidence near the poles because photochemistry cannot account for the distribution without meridional circulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_20-8"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-20">[16]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2007, it was discovered that the upper troposphere of Neptune\'s south pole was about 10 K warmer than the rest of its atmosphere, which averages approximately 73&#160;K (&#8722;200&#160;&#176;C). The temperature differential is enough to let methane, which elsewhere is frozen in the troposphere, escape into the stratosphere near the pole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[77]</a></sup> The relative "hot spot" is due to Neptune\'s <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">axial tilt</a>, which has exposed the south pole to the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> for the last quarter of Neptune\'s year, or roughly 40 Earth years. As Neptune slowly moves towards the opposite side of the Sun, the south pole will be darkened and the north pole illuminated, causing the methane release to shift to the north pole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[78]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Because of seasonal changes, the cloud bands in the southern hemisphere of Neptune have been observed to increase in size and <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a>. This trend was first seen in 1980 and is expected to last until about 2020. The long orbital period of Neptune results in seasons lasting forty years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-villard_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-villard-84">[79]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Storms">Storms</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Neptune%27s_Great_Dark_Spot.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="852" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Neptune%27s_Great_Dark_Spot.jpg/170px-Neptune%27s_Great_Dark_Spot.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Neptune%27s_Great_Dark_Spot.jpg/255px-Neptune%27s_Great_Dark_Spot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Neptune%27s_Great_Dark_Spot.jpg/340px-Neptune%27s_Great_Dark_Spot.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Neptune%27s_Great_Dark_Spot.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Great Dark Spot, as imaged by <i>Voyager 2</i></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1989, the <a href="/wiki/Great_Dark_Spot" title="Great Dark Spot">Great Dark Spot</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Anticyclone" title="Anticyclone">anti-cyclonic</a> storm system spanning 13,000&#160;&#215;&#160;6,600&#160;km,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spot_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-spot-81">[76]</a></sup> was discovered by <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>\'s <i>Voyager 2</i> spacecraft. The storm resembled the <a href="/wiki/Great_Red_Spot" title="Great Red Spot">Great Red Spot</a> of Jupiter. Some five years later, on 2 November 1994, the <i><a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a></i> did not see the Great Dark Spot on the planet. Instead, a new storm similar to the Great Dark Spot was found in Neptune\'s northern hemisphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[80]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Scooter is another storm, a white cloud group farther south than the Great Dark Spot. This nickname first arose during the months leading up to the <i>Voyager 2</i> encounter in 1989, when they were observed moving at speeds faster than the <a href="/wiki/Great_Dark_Spot" title="Great Dark Spot">Great Dark Spot</a> (and images acquired later would subsequently reveal the presence of clouds moving even faster than those that had initially been detected by <i>Voyager 2</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burgess2_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-burgess2-80">[75]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Small_Dark_Spot" title="Small Dark Spot">Small Dark Spot</a> is a southern cyclonic storm, the second-most-intense storm observed during the 1989 encounter. It was initially completely dark, but as <i>Voyager 2</i> approached the planet, a bright core developed and can be seen in most of the highest-resolution images.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[81]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Neptune%E2%80%99s_shrinking_vortex.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="572" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Neptune%E2%80%99s_shrinking_vortex.jpg/220px-Neptune%E2%80%99s_shrinking_vortex.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Neptune%E2%80%99s_shrinking_vortex.jpg/330px-Neptune%E2%80%99s_shrinking_vortex.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Neptune%E2%80%99s_shrinking_vortex.jpg/440px-Neptune%E2%80%99s_shrinking_vortex.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Neptune%E2%80%99s_shrinking_vortex.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Neptune\'s shrinking vortex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[82]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Neptune\'s dark spots are thought to occur in the <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a> at lower altitudes than the brighter cloud features,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[83]</a></sup> so they appear as holes in the upper cloud decks. As they are stable features that can persist for several months, they are thought to be <a href="/wiki/Vortex" title="Vortex">vortex</a> structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apj125_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-apj125-69">[64]</a></sup> Often associated with dark spots are brighter, persistent methane clouds that form around the <a href="/wiki/Tropopause" title="Tropopause">tropopause</a> layer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[84]</a></sup> The persistence of companion clouds shows that some former dark spots may continue to exist as cyclones even though they are no longer visible as a dark feature. Dark spots may dissipate when they migrate too close to the equator or possibly through some other unknown mechanism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[85]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_heating">Internal heating</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_four_sides_of_Neptune_(captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope).tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2474" data-file-width="2250" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/The_four_sides_of_Neptune_%28captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope%29.tif/lossy-page1-170px-The_four_sides_of_Neptune_%28captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope%29.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/The_four_sides_of_Neptune_%28captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope%29.tif/lossy-page1-255px-The_four_sides_of_Neptune_%28captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope%29.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/The_four_sides_of_Neptune_%28captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope%29.tif/lossy-page1-340px-The_four_sides_of_Neptune_%28captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope%29.tif.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_four_sides_of_Neptune_(captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope).tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Four images taken a few hours apart with the NASA/ESA <i>Hubble Space Telescope</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> Wide Field Camera 3<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[86]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Neptune\'s more varied weather when compared to Uranus is due in part to its higher <a href="/wiki/Internal_heating" title="Internal heating">internal heating</a>. Although Neptune lies over 50% farther from the Sun than Uranus, and receives only 40% its amount of sunlight,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lunine_1993_20-9"><a href="#cite_note-Lunine_1993-20">[16]</a></sup> the two planets\' surface temperatures are roughly equal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-williams_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-williams-92">[87]</a></sup> The upper regions of Neptune\'s troposphere reach a low temperature of 51.8&#160;K (&#8722;221.3&#160;&#176;C). At a depth where the <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure" title="Atmospheric pressure">atmospheric pressure</a> equals 1 <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">bar</a> (100&#160;<a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">kPa</a>), the temperature is 72.00&#160;K (&#8722;201.15&#160;&#176;C).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[88]</a></sup> Deeper inside the layers of gas, the temperature rises steadily. As with Uranus, the source of this heating is unknown, but the discrepancy is larger: Uranus only radiates 1.1 times as much energy as it receives from the Sun;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[89]</a></sup> whereas Neptune radiates about 2.61 times as much energy as it receives from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[90]</a></sup> Neptune is the farthest planet from the Sun, yet its internal energy is sufficient to drive the fastest planetary winds seen in the Solar System. Depending on the thermal properties of its interior, the heat left over from Neptune\'s formation may be sufficient to explain its current heat flow, though it is more difficult to simultaneously explain <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>\'s lack of internal heat while preserving the apparent similarity between the two planets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[91]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit_and_rotation">Orbit and rotation</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Neptune_Orbit.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="248" data-file-width="248" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Neptune_Orbit.gif/220px-Neptune_Orbit.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/66/Neptune_Orbit.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Neptune_Orbit.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Neptune (red arc) completes one orbit around the Sun (centre) for every 164.79 orbits of Earth. The light blue object represents Uranus.</div></div></div>\n<p>The average distance between Neptune and the Sun is <span class="nowrap">4.5 billion km</span> (about 30.1&#160;<a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical units</a> (AU)), and it completes an orbit on average every 164.79&#160;years, subject to a variability of around &#177;0.1&#160;years. The perihelion distance is 29.81&#160;AU; the aphelion distance is 30.33&#160;AU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AA_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-AA-97">[92]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 11 July 2011, Neptune completed its first full <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barycentric_coordinates_(astronomy)" title="Barycentric coordinates (astronomy)">barycentric</a> orbit since its discovery in 1846,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-obsjul711_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-obsjul711-98">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-azureworld_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-azureworld-99">[94]</a></sup> although it did not appear at its exact discovery position in the sky, because Earth was in a different location in its 365.26-day orbit. Because of the motion of the Sun in relation to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barycentre#Barycenter_in_astronomy" title="Barycentre">barycentre</a> of the Solar System, on 11 July Neptune was also not at its exact discovery position in relation to the Sun; if the more common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliocentric" title="Heliocentric">heliocentric</a> coordinate system is used, the discovery longitude was reached on 12 July 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact2_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-fact2-10">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UT2010-08_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-UT2010-08-100">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horizons2011_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Horizons2011-101">[96]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The elliptical orbit of Neptune is inclined 1.77&#176; compared to that of Earth.\n</p><p>The axial tilt of Neptune is 28.32&#176;,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[97]</a></sup> which is similar to the tilts of Earth (23&#176;) and Mars (25&#176;). As a result, Neptune experiences similar seasonal changes to Earth. The long orbital period of Neptune means that the seasons last for forty Earth years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-villard_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-villard-84">[79]</a></sup> Its sidereal rotation period (day) is roughly 16.11&#160;hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact2_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-fact2-10">[8]</a></sup> Because its axial tilt is comparable to Earth\'s, the variation in the length of its day over the course of its long year is not any more extreme.\n</p><p>Because Neptune is not a solid body, its atmosphere undergoes <a href="/wiki/Differential_rotation" title="Differential rotation">differential rotation</a>. The wide equatorial zone rotates with a period of about 18&#160;hours, which is slower than the 16.1-hour rotation of the planet\'s magnetic field. By contrast, the reverse is true for the polar regions where the rotation period is 12&#160;hours. This differential rotation is the most pronounced of any planet in the Solar System,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[98]</a></sup> and it results in strong latitudinal wind shear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-apj125_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-apj125-69">[64]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:left;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbital_resonances">Orbital resonances</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Resonant_trans-Neptunian_object" title="Resonant trans-Neptunian object">resonant trans-Neptunian object</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neptune_trojan" title="Neptune trojan">Neptune trojan</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TheKuiperBelt_classes-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="320" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/TheKuiperBelt_classes-en.svg/220px-TheKuiperBelt_classes-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/TheKuiperBelt_classes-en.svg/330px-TheKuiperBelt_classes-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/TheKuiperBelt_classes-en.svg/440px-TheKuiperBelt_classes-en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TheKuiperBelt_classes-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A diagram showing the major orbital resonances in the <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a> caused by Neptune: the highlighted regions are the 2:3 resonance (plutinos), the nonresonant <a href="/wiki/Classical_Kuiper_belt_object" title="Classical Kuiper belt object">"classical belt"</a> (cubewanos), and the 1:2 resonance (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Twotino" title="Twotino">twotinos</a>).</div></div></div>\n<p>Neptune\'s orbit has a profound impact on the region directly beyond it, known as the <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a>. The Kuiper belt is a ring of small icy worlds, similar to the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a> but far larger, extending from Neptune\'s orbit at 30&#160;AU out to about 55&#160;AU from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[99]</a></sup> Much in the same way that Jupiter\'s gravity dominates the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>, shaping its structure, so Neptune\'s gravity dominates the Kuiper belt. Over the age of the Solar System, certain regions of the Kuiper belt became destabilised by Neptune\'s gravity, creating gaps in the Kuiper belt\'s structure. The region between 40 and 42&#160;AU is an example.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[100]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There do exist orbits within these empty regions where objects can survive for the age of the Solar System. These <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">resonances</a> occur when Neptune\'s orbital period is a precise fraction of that of the object, such as 1:2, or 3:4. If, say, an object orbits the Sun once for every two Neptune orbits, it will only complete half an orbit by the time Neptune returns to its original position. The most heavily populated resonance in the Kuiper belt, with over 200 known objects,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[101]</a></sup> is the 2:3 resonance. Objects in this resonance complete 2 orbits for every 3 of Neptune, and are known as <a href="/wiki/Plutino" title="Plutino">plutinos</a> because the largest of the known Kuiper belt objects, <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>, is among them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[102]</a></sup> Although Pluto crosses Neptune\'s orbit regularly, the 2:3 resonance ensures they can never collide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[103]</a></sup> The 3:4, 3:5, 4:7 and 2:5 resonances are less populated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[104]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Neptune has a number of known <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trojan_(astronomy)" title="Trojan (astronomy)">trojan objects</a> occupying both the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>&#8211;Neptune <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point#L4_and_L5" title="Lagrangian point">L<sub>4</sub></a> and <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point#L4_and_L5" title="Lagrangian point">L<sub>5</sub></a> <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point" title="Lagrangian point">Lagrangian points</a>&#8212;gravitationally stable regions leading and trailing Neptune in its orbit, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[105]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Neptune_trojan" title="Neptune trojan">Neptune trojans</a> can be viewed as being in a 1:1 resonance with Neptune. Some Neptune trojans are remarkably stable in their orbits, and are likely to have formed alongside Neptune rather than being captured. The first object identified as associated with Neptune\'s trailing <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point#L4_and_L5" title="Lagrangian point">L<sub>5</sub></a> <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point" title="Lagrangian point">Lagrangian point</a> was <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2008_LC18" title="2008 LC18">2008 LC<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">18</span></span></a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sheppard_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sheppard-111">[106]</a></sup> Neptune also has a temporary <a href="/wiki/Quasi-satellite" title="Quasi-satellite">quasi-satellite</a>, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(309239)_2007_RW10" title="(309239) 2007 RW10">(309239) 2007 RW<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">10</span></span></a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-quasi_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-quasi-112">[107]</a></sup> The object has been a quasi-satellite of Neptune for about 12,500 years and it will remain in that dynamical state for another 12,500 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-quasi_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-quasi-112">[107]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_and_migration">Formation and migration</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">Formation and evolution of the Solar System</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nice_model" title="Nice model">Nice model</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lhborbits.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="454" data-file-width="1360" decoding="async" height="73" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Lhborbits.png/220px-Lhborbits.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Lhborbits.png/330px-Lhborbits.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Lhborbits.png/440px-Lhborbits.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lhborbits.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A simulation showing the outer planets and Kuiper belt: a) before Jupiter and Saturn reached a 2:1 resonance; b) after inward scattering of Kuiper belt objects following the orbital shift of Neptune; c) after ejection of scattered Kuiper belt bodies by Jupiter</div></div></div>\n<p>The formation of the ice giants, Neptune and Uranus, has proven difficult to model precisely. Current models suggest that the matter density in the outer regions of the Solar System was too low to account for the formation of such large bodies from the traditionally accepted method of core <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">accretion</a>, and various hypotheses have been advanced to explain their formation. One is that the ice giants were not formed by core accretion but from instabilities within the original <a href="/wiki/Protoplanetary_disk" title="Protoplanetary disk">protoplanetary disc</a> and later had their atmospheres blasted away by radiation from a nearby massive <a href="/wiki/OB_star" title="OB star">OB star</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boss_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Boss-56">[51]</a></sup>\n</p><p>An alternative concept is that they formed closer to the Sun, where the matter density was higher, and then subsequently <a href="/wiki/Planetary_migration" title="Planetary migration">migrated</a> to their current orbits after the removal of the gaseous protoplanetary disc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[108]</a></sup> This hypothesis of migration after formation is favoured, due to its ability to better explain the occupancy of the populations of small objects observed in the trans-Neptunian region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[109]</a></sup> The current most widely accepted<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crida2009_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crida2009-115">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Desch07_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Desch07-116">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith2009_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith2009-117">[112]</a></sup> explanation of the details of this hypothesis is known as the <a href="/wiki/Nice_model" title="Nice model">Nice model</a>, which explores the effect of a migrating Neptune and the other giant planets on the structure of the Kuiper belt.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Moons">Moons</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Neptune" title="Moons of Neptune">Moons of Neptune</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For a timeline of discovery dates, see <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_discovery_of_Solar_System_planets_and_their_moons" title="Timeline of discovery of Solar System planets and their moons">Timeline of discovery of Solar System planets and their moons</a>.</div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Neptune-visible.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="403" data-file-width="315" decoding="async" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Neptune-visible.jpg/170px-Neptune-visible.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Neptune-visible.jpg/255px-Neptune-visible.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/63/Neptune-visible.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Neptune-visible.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Natural-colour view of Neptune with Proteus (top), Larissa (lower right), and Despina (left), from the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Neptune has 14 known <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_4-14"><a href="#cite_note-fact-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[113]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a> is the largest Neptunian moon, comprising more than 99.5% of the mass in orbit around Neptune,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[f]</a></sup> and it is the only one massive enough to be <a href="/wiki/Spheroid" title="Spheroid">spheroidal</a>. <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a> was discovered by <a href="/wiki/William_Lassell" title="William Lassell">William Lassell</a> just 17&#160;days after the discovery of Neptune itself. Unlike all other large planetary moons in the Solar System, Triton has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrograde_orbit" title="Retrograde orbit">retrograde orbit</a>, indicating that it was captured rather than forming in place; it was probably once a <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a> in the Kuiper belt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[114]</a></sup> It is close enough to Neptune to be locked into a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synchronous_rotation" title="Synchronous rotation">synchronous rotation</a>, and it is slowly spiralling inward because of <a href="/wiki/Tidal_acceleration" title="Tidal acceleration">tidal acceleration</a>. It will eventually be torn apart, in about 3.6&#160;billion years, when it reaches the <a href="/wiki/Roche_limit" title="Roche limit">Roche limit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[115]</a></sup> In 1989, Triton was the coldest object that had yet been measured in the Solar System,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[116]</a></sup> with estimated temperatures of 38&#160;K (&#8722;235&#160;&#176;C).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[117]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Neptune\'s second known satellite (by order of discovery), the irregular moon <a href="/wiki/Nereid_(moon)" title="Nereid (moon)">Nereid</a>, has one of the most eccentric orbits of any satellite in the Solar System. The eccentricity of 0.7512 gives it an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apoapsis" title="Apoapsis">apoapsis</a> that is seven times its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periapsis" title="Periapsis">periapsis</a> distance from Neptune.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[g]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Proteus_(Voyager_2).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="840" data-file-width="836" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Proteus_%28Voyager_2%29.jpg/170px-Proteus_%28Voyager_2%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Proteus_%28Voyager_2%29.jpg/255px-Proteus_%28Voyager_2%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Proteus_%28Voyager_2%29.jpg/340px-Proteus_%28Voyager_2%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Proteus_(Voyager_2).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Neptune\'s moon <a href="/wiki/Proteus_(moon)" title="Proteus (moon)">Proteus</a></div></div></div>\n<p>From July to September 1989, <i>Voyager 2</i> discovered six moons of Neptune.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science4936-2_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-science4936-2-125">[118]</a></sup> Of these, the irregularly shaped <a href="/wiki/Proteus_(moon)" title="Proteus (moon)">Proteus</a> is notable for being as large as a body of its density can be without being pulled into a spherical shape by its own gravity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-126">[119]</a></sup> Although the second-most-massive Neptunian moon, it is only 0.25% the mass of Triton. Neptune\'s innermost four moons&#8212;<a href="/wiki/Naiad_(moon)" title="Naiad (moon)">Naiad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thalassa_(moon)" title="Thalassa (moon)">Thalassa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Despina_(moon)" title="Despina (moon)">Despina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Galatea_(moon)" title="Galatea (moon)">Galatea</a>&#8212;orbit close enough to be within Neptune\'s rings. The next-farthest out, <a href="/wiki/Larissa_(moon)" title="Larissa (moon)">Larissa</a>, was originally discovered in 1981 when it had occulted a star. This occultation had been attributed to ring arcs, but when <i>Voyager 2</i> observed Neptune in 1989, Larissa was found to have caused it. Five new irregular moons discovered between 2002 and 2003 were announced in 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HolmanKavelaarsGrav2004_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-HolmanKavelaarsGrav2004-127">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[121]</a></sup>  A new moon and the smallest yet, <a href="/wiki/Neptune_XIV" title="Neptune XIV">Neptune XIV</a>, was found in 2013. Because Neptune was the Roman god of the sea, Neptune\'s moons have been named after lesser sea gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGS_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-USGS-42">[38]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Planetary_rings">Planetary rings</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Neptune" title="Rings of Neptune">Rings of Neptune</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Neptunerings.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="258" data-file-width="300" decoding="async" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Neptunerings.jpg/220px-Neptunerings.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/87/Neptunerings.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Neptunerings.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Neptune\'s rings</div></div></div>\n<p>Neptune has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planetary_ring" title="Planetary ring">planetary ring</a> system, though one much less substantial than that of <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn" title="Rings of Saturn">Saturn</a>. The rings may consist of ice particles coated with silicates or carbon-based material, which most likely gives them a reddish hue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[122]</a></sup>\nThe three main rings are the narrow Adams Ring, 63,000&#160;km from the centre of Neptune, the Le Verrier Ring, at 53,000&#160;km, and the broader, fainter Galle Ring, at 42,000&#160;km. A faint outward extension to the Le Verrier Ring has been named Lassell; it is bounded at its outer edge by the Arago Ring at 57,000&#160;km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[123]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first of these planetary rings was detected in 1968 by a team led by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Guinan" title="Edward Guinan">Edward Guinan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ring1_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-ring1-25">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[124]</a></sup> In the early 1980s, analysis of this data along with newer observations led to the hypothesis that this ring might be incomplete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[125]</a></sup>\nEvidence that the rings might have gaps first arose during a <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">stellar occultation</a> in 1984 when the rings obscured a star on immersion but not on emersion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicholson90_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholson90-133">[126]</a></sup> Images from <i>Voyager 2</i> in 1989 settled the issue by showing several faint rings.\n</p><p>The outermost ring, Adams, contains five prominent arcs now named <i>Courage</i>, <i>Libert&#233;</i>, <i>Egalit&#233;&#160;1</i>, <i>Egalit&#233;&#160;2</i> and <i>Fraternit&#233;</i> (Courage, Liberty, Equality and Fraternity).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[127]</a></sup> The existence of arcs was difficult to explain because the laws of motion would predict that arcs would spread out into a uniform ring over short timescales. Astronomers now estimate that the arcs are corralled into their current form by the gravitational effects of <a href="/wiki/Galatea_(moon)" title="Galatea (moon)">Galatea</a>, a moon just inward from the ring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[129]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Earth-based observations announced in 2005 appeared to show that Neptune\'s rings are much more unstable than previously thought. Images taken from the <a href="/wiki/W._M._Keck_Observatory" title="W. M. Keck Observatory">W. M. Keck Observatory</a> in 2002 and 2003 show considerable decay in the rings when compared to images by <i>Voyager 2</i>. In particular, it seems that the <i>Libert&#233;</i> arc might disappear in as little as one century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[130]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:left;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observation">Observation</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Neptune_from_the_VLT_with_MUSE_GALACSI_Narrow_Field_Mode_adaptive_optics.jpg"><img alt="Neptune visible in the night sky" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1246" data-file-width="1208" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Neptune_from_the_VLT_with_MUSE_GALACSI_Narrow_Field_Mode_adaptive_optics.jpg/220px-Neptune_from_the_VLT_with_MUSE_GALACSI_Narrow_Field_Mode_adaptive_optics.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Neptune_from_the_VLT_with_MUSE_GALACSI_Narrow_Field_Mode_adaptive_optics.jpg/330px-Neptune_from_the_VLT_with_MUSE_GALACSI_Narrow_Field_Mode_adaptive_optics.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Neptune_from_the_VLT_with_MUSE_GALACSI_Narrow_Field_Mode_adaptive_optics.jpg/440px-Neptune_from_the_VLT_with_MUSE_GALACSI_Narrow_Field_Mode_adaptive_optics.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Neptune_from_the_VLT_with_MUSE_GALACSI_Narrow_Field_Mode_adaptive_optics.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In 2018, the <a href="/wiki/European_Southern_Observatory" title="European Southern Observatory">European Southern Observatory</a> developed unique laser-based methods to get clear and high-resolution images of Neptune from the surface of Earth.</div></div></div>\n<p>Neptune brightened significantly between 1980 and 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmude_et_al_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schmude_et_al-138">[131]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">apparent magnitude</a> currently ranges from 7.67 to 7.89 with a mean of 7.78 and a standard deviation of 0.06.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mallama_and_Hilton_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mallama_and_Hilton-15">[12]</a></sup> Prior to 1980 the planet was as faint as magnitude 8.0.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mallama_and_Hilton_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mallama_and_Hilton-15">[12]</a></sup> Neptune is too faint to be visible to the <a href="/wiki/Naked_eye" title="Naked eye">naked eye</a> and can be outshone by Jupiter\'s <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean moons</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a> <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a> <a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">4 Vesta</a>, <a href="/wiki/2_Pallas" title="2 Pallas">2 Pallas</a>, <a href="/wiki/7_Iris" title="7 Iris">7 Iris</a>, <a href="/wiki/3_Juno" title="3 Juno">3 Juno</a>, and <a href="/wiki/6_Hebe" title="6 Hebe">6 Hebe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[132]</a></sup> A telescope or strong binoculars will resolve Neptune as a small blue disk, similar in appearance to Uranus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[133]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Because of the distance of Neptune from Earth, its <a href="/wiki/Angular_diameter" title="Angular diameter">angular diameter</a> only ranges from 2.2 to 2.4&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arcsecond" title="Arcsecond">arcseconds</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fact_4-15"><a href="#cite_note-fact-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ephemeris_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-ephemeris-16">[13]</a></sup> the smallest of the Solar System planets. Its small apparent size makes it challenging to study visually. Most telescopic data was fairly limited until the advent of the <i><a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a></i> and large ground-based telescopes with <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_optics" title="Adaptive optics">adaptive optics</a> (AO).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[136]</a></sup>\nThe first scientifically useful observation of Neptune from ground-based telescopes using adaptive optics, was commenced in 1997 from Hawaii.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[137]</a></sup> Neptune is currently entering its spring and summer season and has been shown to be heating up, with increased atmospheric activity and brightness as a consequence. Combined with technological advancements, ground-based telescopes with adaptive optics are recording increasingly more detailed images of it. Both <i>Hubble</i> and the adaptive-optics telescopes on Earth have made many new discoveries within the Solar System since the mid-1990s, with a large increase in the number of known satellites and moons around the outer planet, among others. In 2004 and 2005, five new small satellites of Neptune with diameters between 38 and 61 kilometres were discovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[138]</a></sup>\n</p><p>From Earth, Neptune goes through <a href="/wiki/Apparent_retrograde_motion" title="Apparent retrograde motion">apparent retrograde motion</a> every 367&#160;days, resulting in a looping motion against the background stars during each <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Opposition_(astronomy)" title="Opposition (astronomy)">opposition</a>. These loops carried it close to the 1846 discovery coordinates in April and July 2010 and again in October and November 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horizons2011_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-Horizons2011-101">[96]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Observation of Neptune in the radio-frequency band shows that it is a source of both continuous emission and irregular bursts. Both sources are thought to originate from its rotating magnetic field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elkins-tanton_68-4"><a href="#cite_note-elkins-tanton-68">[63]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> part of the spectrum, Neptune\'s storms appear bright against the cooler background, allowing the size and shape of these features to be readily tracked.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[139]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration">Exploration</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Exploration_of_Neptune" title="Exploration of Neptune">Exploration of Neptune</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Triton_moon_mosaic_Voyager_2_(large).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4700" data-file-width="4700" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Triton_moon_mosaic_Voyager_2_%28large%29.jpg/220px-Triton_moon_mosaic_Voyager_2_%28large%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Triton_moon_mosaic_Voyager_2_%28large%29.jpg/330px-Triton_moon_mosaic_Voyager_2_%28large%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Triton_moon_mosaic_Voyager_2_%28large%29.jpg/440px-Triton_moon_mosaic_Voyager_2_%28large%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Triton_moon_mosaic_Voyager_2_(large).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <i>Voyager 2</i> mosaic of Triton</div></div></div>\n<p><i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a></i> is the only spacecraft that has visited Neptune. The spacecraft<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> closest approach to the planet occurred on 25 August 1989. Because this was the last major planet the spacecraft could visit, it was decided to make a close flyby of the moon Triton, regardless of the consequences to the trajectory, similarly to what was done for <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a></i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s encounter with <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> and its moon <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>. The images relayed back to Earth from <i>Voyager 2</i> became the basis of a 1989 <a href="/wiki/PBS" title="PBS">PBS</a> all-night program, <i>Neptune All Night</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[140]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the encounter, signals from the spacecraft required 246&#160;minutes to reach Earth. Hence, for the most part, <i>Voyager 2</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> mission relied on preloaded commands for the Neptune encounter. The spacecraft performed a near-encounter with the moon <a href="/wiki/Nereid_(moon)" title="Nereid (moon)">Nereid</a> before it came within 4,400&#160;km of Neptune\'s atmosphere on 25 August, then passed close to the planet\'s largest moon <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a> later the same day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burgess_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-burgess-148">[141]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The spacecraft verified the existence of a magnetic field surrounding the planet and discovered that the field was offset from the centre and tilted in a manner similar to the field around Uranus. Neptune\'s rotation period was determined using measurements of radio emissions and <i>Voyager 2</i> also showed that Neptune had a surprisingly active weather system. Six new moons were discovered, and the planet was shown to have more than one ring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-science4936-2_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-science4936-2-125">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burgess_148-1"><a href="#cite_note-burgess-148">[141]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The flyby also provided the first accurate measurement of Neptune\'s mass which was found to be 0.5 percent less than previously calculated. The new figure disproved the hypothesis that an undiscovered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planet_X" title="Planet X">Planet X</a> acted upon the orbits of Neptune and Uranus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[143]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the <i>Voyager 2</i> flyby mission, the next step in scientific exploration of the Neptunian system, is considered to be a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flagship_Program" title="Flagship Program">Flagship orbital mission</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flagship_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Flagship-151">[144]</a></sup> Such a hypothetical mission is envisioned to be possible in the late 2020s or early 2030s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flagship_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-Flagship-151">[144]</a></sup>  However, there have been discussions to launch Neptune missions sooner. In 2003, there was a proposal in <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>\'s "Vision Missions Studies" for a "<a href="/wiki/Neptune_Orbiter" title="Neptune Orbiter">Neptune Orbiter with Probes</a>" mission that does <i><a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini&#8211;Huygens">Cassini</a></i>-level science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[145]</a></sup> Another, more recent proposal was for <i><a href="/wiki/Argo_(spacecraft)" title="Argo (spacecraft)">Argo</a></i>, a flyby spacecraft to be launched in 2019, that would visit <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>, Neptune, and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt_object" title="Kuiper belt object">Kuiper belt object</a>. The focus would be on Neptune and its largest moon <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a> to be investigated around 2029.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-argho1_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-argho1-153">[146]</a></sup> The proposed <i><a href="/wiki/New_Horizons_2" title="New Horizons 2">New Horizons 2</a></i> mission (which was later scrapped) might also have done a close flyby of the Neptunian system.\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>\n<div aria-label="Portals" class="noprint portal plainlist tright" role="navigation" style="margin:0.5em 0 0.5em 1em;border:solid #aaa 1px">\n<ul Neptune">Hot Neptune</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Planets_in_astrology#Neptune" title="Planets in astrology">Neptune in astrology</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Neptunium" title="Neptunium">Neptunium</a></li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Planets" title="The Planets">Neptune, the Mystic</a></i> &#8211; one of the seven movements in <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Holst" title="Gustav Holst">Gustav Holst</a>\'s <i>Planets</i> suite</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_far_future" title="Timeline of the far future">Timeline of the far future</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-barycentre-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-barycentre_7-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Orbital elements refer to the Neptune barycentre and Solar System barycentre. These are the instantaneous <a href="/wiki/Osculating_orbit" title="Osculating orbit">osculating</a> values at the precise <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J2000" title="J2000">J2000</a> epoch. Barycentre quantities are given because, in contrast to the planetary centre, they do not experience appreciable changes on a day-to-day basis from the motion of the moons.</span>\n</li>\n<li atmospheric pressure</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-1bar_b-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1bar_b_11-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Based on the volume
